    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer11282832361675026379">This is where the dragons went. They lie ... not dead, not asleep, but ... dormant. And although the space they occupy isn't like normal space, nevertheless they are packed in tightly. They could put you in mind of a can of sardines, if you thought sardines were huge and scaly. And presumably, somewhere, there's a key...<br/><br/>GUARDS! GUARDS! is the eighth Discworld novel - and a</span>
  <span id="freeText11282832361675026379" style="display:none">This is where the dragons went. They lie ... not dead, not asleep, but ... dormant. And although the space they occupy isn't like normal space, nevertheless they are packed in tightly. They could put you in mind of a can of sardines, if you thought sardines were huge and scaly. And presumably, somewhere, there's a key...<br /><br />GUARDS! GUARDS! is the eighth Discworld novel - and after this, dragons will never be the same again!</span>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I think this would be a fantastic place to start - it's one of the best, and it's first in one of the subseries... the books about the Night Watch. I <a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I think this would be a fantastic place to start - it's one of the best, and it's first in one of the subseries... the books about the Night Watch. I personally would recommend reading each subseries (the Witches, Death, the Night Watch, etc.) in order, but the subseries and the standalones (Pyramids, Small Gods, etc.) can be read in any order.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_904889'><a id="like_count_community_question_904889" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/904889?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">2 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F64216.Guards_Guards_">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/904889-it-isn-t-written-as-a-book-for-children">4 years ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/904889-it-isn-t-written-as-a-book-for-children">Add your answer</a>
</li>
</ul>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/3623332-joseph"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1272164632i/3623332._UX100_CR0,1,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/3623332-joseph">Joseph</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">It's intended audience is adult. There are some adult themes that are not explicitly written about. There are subtleties that might be missed by young<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">It's intended audience is adult. There are some adult themes that are not explicitly written about. There are subtleties that might be missed by younger readers. I just re-read the book, and it still has as much relevance as it did when it first came out. <br /><br />If looking for something Terry Pratchett for younger readers, try Wee Free Men. It's just as good, but is intended for a younger audience.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

<div id="review_61529898" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/61529898" />
    <a title="Patrick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/922495-patrick"><img alt="Patrick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1351307095p2/922495.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/61529898?book_show_action=true">Jun 29, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Patrick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Patrick" href="/user/show/922495-patrick">Patrick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer61529898" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4136290310654135582">I've been re-reading some of the older Pratchett novels. Partly because I simply enjoy them. Partly because they help keep me sane in an increasingly infuriating world. And partly because they are comfortable to me. The literary equivalent of a security blanket. <br/><br/>But I'll be honest, I'm also reading them so I can postpone reading the final Pratchett novel that came out a little while ago. It will be hard for me to read that, knowing that afterwards, there will be no more new Pratchett to read. Ev</span>
  <span id="freeText4136290310654135582" style="display:none">I've been re-reading some of the older Pratchett novels. Partly because I simply enjoy them. Partly because they help keep me sane in an increasingly infuriating world. And partly because they are comfortable to me. The literary equivalent of a security blanket. <br /><br />But I'll be honest, I'm also reading them so I can postpone reading the final Pratchett novel that came out a little while ago. It will be hard for me to read that, knowing that afterwards, there will be no more new Pratchett to read. Ever. <br /><br />This is the book that introduces Vimes, perhaps my favorite Discworld character. And that's saying something, given the number of books in the series, and the number of truly delightful characters. <br /><br />That said, on re-reading this, I was surprised to see where Vimes got his start. The Vimes in this first book is a good character, but it's not the character I grew to love. It's pretty obvious that Pratchett was planning on him being a Discworld version of the down-on-his-luck gumshoe detective. Hard-drinking and bitter. More stubborn than clever.<br /><br />Don't get me wrong. Vimes is a good character in this book, but he's not particularly noteworthy. Carrot and the Patrician shine more.<br /><br />As a writer, it's interesting for me to see how Pratchett started here, with a functional but not stellar character, who he then changed (rather abruptly) into one of my favorites in the whole series. It's nice for me to see that Pratchett didn't have it all planned out ahead of time, and that he was, in fact, just making it all up as he went along. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4136290310654135582" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1062882955" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1062882955" />
    <a title="Deborah Markus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/954028-deborah-markus"><img alt="Deborah Markus" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1062882955?book_show_action=true">Sep 23, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Deborah Markus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Deborah Markus" href="/user/show/954028-deborah-markus">Deborah Markus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1062882955" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2703341813223317737">How To Read Terry Pratchett's Discworld Novels<br/><br/><b>1. Skip the first two books in the series.</b> This is crucial. If you read these first, you will be rightly annoyed by their just-barely-okayness, and spend the rest of your life muttering about how overrated he is. <br/><br/>Terry Pratchett wrote these two books. Then all of a sudden he started writing really, really well. I don't know what happened. Maybe he sold his soul. My own theory is that his long-suffering wife waited as patiently as she could for him to</span>
  <span id="freeText2703341813223317737" style="display:none">How To Read Terry Pratchett's Discworld Novels<br /><br /><b>1. Skip the first two books in the series.</b> This is crucial. If you read these first, you will be rightly annoyed by their just-barely-okayness, and spend the rest of your life muttering about how overrated he is. <br /><br />Terry Pratchett wrote these two books. Then all of a sudden he started writing really, really well. I don't know what happened. Maybe he sold his soul. My own theory is that his long-suffering wife waited as patiently as she could for him to pull himself together as a writer; and then, after she proofread his second novel, something in her snapped and she thwapped him in the head with the book in question. "You're better than this, damn it!" she shouted. "Now get in there and <i>write something good!"</i> And he did.<br /><br /><b>2. Start with the first book in one of the subseries'.</b> The Discworld books all take place on the same planet, but they involve different groups of people:<br /><br />The witches (my favorites)<br />The Night Watch (my also-favorites)<br />Death (granted, he's not a group, but he does have a family)<br />The wizards<br /><br />Okay, there are standalones, like <i>Small Gods</i>. And in a way, <i>all</i> the Discworld books are standalones. (Except for the first two, because the first one ends on a cliffhanger. Like I said, skip them until you've read some of the really good ones, and then brace yourself and read them purely for continuity purposes.) I started with the 23rd book in the series, <i>Carpe Jugulum,</i> and was perfectly fine, other than a tendency to preach the gospel of Discworld Is Flippin' Amazing And You <i>Have</i> To Read These Books, which I'm trying to get under control.<br /><br />But it's a lot of fun to follow the threads. To start, for instance, with <i>Equal Rites</i>, which introduces you to both the witches <i>and</i> the wizards. (This isn't Hogwarts, and those most definitely are <i>not</i> the same thing in Discworld.) Then you can go on to <i>Wyrd Sisters</i> and <i>Witches Abroad</i>, and fall absolutely in love with Granny Weatherwax and Nanny Ogg. (I fully intend to be Granny Weatherwax when I grow up, right down to the goat farm and the ability to terrify people just by saying "Good morning." Watch this space.)<br /><br />Or you can start with <i>Guards! Guards!</i>, and be introduced to the men of the Night Watch. Just bear in mind that I'm first in line if Captain Vimes becomes available, so keep your inevitable crush on him to yourself.<br /><br />I posted approximately 17,000 updates while I reread this book, all quotable quotes. That's not even all the bits I <i>wanted</i> to post, but Goodreads has a strict word-count limit on updates because Goodreads h̶a̶t̶e̶s̶ ̶m̶e̶ knows how I get.<br /><br />You can get a perfectly good sense of whether or not you'll enjoy <i>Guards! Guards!</i> by perusing said updates. You will enjoy it if you already enjoy fantasy; you will very likely enjoy it even if you don't generally enjoy fantasy, because the humor, characterization, and dialogue are ridiculously good.<br /><br />Have a good time. And remember – hands off Vimes. He's mine.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2703341813223317737" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_101060202" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/101060202" />
    <a title="Nataliya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3672777-nataliya"><img alt="Nataliya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605995014p2/3672777.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/101060202?book_show_action=true">May 02, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nataliya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nataliya" href="/user/show/3672777-nataliya">Nataliya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=pratchett">pratchett</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=2013-reads">2013-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=2015-reads">2015-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=2020-reads">2020-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer101060202" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8520798807135465352">What I love most about Pratchett's books is that under a thin layer of funny footnotes-peppered pun-heavy parody lies <b>the core of deep seriousness rooted in the quite sobering understanding of the shallow pettiness of human mundanity fueled by jealousy, bile, spite, and closemindedness.</b><blockquote>
  <i>"There was a thoughtful pause in the conversation as the assembled Brethren mentally divided the universe into the deserving and the undeserving, and put themselves on the appropriate side."</i>
</blockquote>In the end, the onl</span>
  <span id="freeText8520798807135465352" style="display:none">What I love most about Pratchett's books is that under a thin layer of funny footnotes-peppered pun-heavy parody lies <b>the core of deep seriousness rooted in the quite sobering understanding of the shallow pettiness of human mundanity fueled by jealousy, bile, spite, and closemindedness.</b><blockquote>
  <i>"There was a thoughtful pause in the conversation as the assembled Brethren mentally divided the universe into the deserving and the undeserving, and put themselves on the appropriate side."</i>
</blockquote>In the end, the only thing that makes it possible to wake up in the morning is just a slight deviation from this depressing state of affairs that leads to the understanding and a bit of dignity and doing what's right - even when that's not quite expected. <b>Because someone has to uphold what's right. Because someone needs to swim against the current.</b> <blockquote>
<i>He shrugged. "They're just people," he said. "They're just doing what people do. Sir."</i><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380334544i/693821.jpg" width="350" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> </blockquote>This is one of my favorite Discworld books and one of the best starting points for the Discworld newbies. It is the first book in the subcycle focusing on <b>the City Watch of Ankh Morpork</b> (the Pearl of Cities¹ on the Disc); the book that really takes a look into the inner workings of this crowded, dirty and despicable and yet lovably tenacious urban metropolis:<blockquote>¹ <i>"Ankh-Morpork! Pearl of cities! This is not a completely accurate description, of course — it was not round and shiny — but even its worst enemies would agree that if you had to liken Ankh-Morpork to anything, then it might as well be a piece of rubbish covered with the diseased secretions of a dying mollusc."</i>
</blockquote>In <b>Pratchett's tradition of deconstructing the tropes</b> this one has its kings and tyrants and secret societies and dragons and maidens and heroes and heirs with birthmarks and magical swords, as well as million-to-one chances - except that things tend to not work out as planned. And all of it is woven into a neat tight plot that carries us through the dry humor and slapstick and sad seriousness to the unexpected depth as you allow the cogs and wheels of your brain turn contemplating Pratchett's intentions.<br>---------------<br><blockquote>
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1600316855i/30116945._SX540_.jpg" width="400" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
  <br>
  <br>
  <i>"If there was anything that depressed him more than his own cynicism, it was that quite often it still wasn't as cynical as real life."</i>
</blockquote><b>I am a Sam Vimes girl through and through.</b> And this book is our introduction to our cynical (anti)hero, uncompromising, grumpy and terrifyingly sober/knurd <i>('knurd' as in complete and utter opposite to drunk, beyond simple sobriety)</i> Ankh-Morpork copper with the <b>unsettling tendency to ask uncomfortable questions when others would much rather he didn't.</b> <br><br>This is the beginning of the Vimes we all know and love, the evolution from the down-in-the-gutter pessimistic drunk "brung low by a woman"¹ (the city itself, that is) to the equally pessimistic servant not of the King or the Patrician but the law, a thorn in the side of so many not-so-uncorrupt but quite respectably upstanding Ankh citizens.<blockquote>¹ <i>"The city wasa, wasa, wasa wossname. Thing. </i>Woman. <i>That's what it was. Woman. Roaring, ancient, centuries old. Strung you along, let you fall in thingy, love, then kicked you inna, inna, thingy. Thingy, in your mouth. Tongue. Tonsils. </i>Teeth.<i> That's what it, she, did. She wasa ... thing, you know, lady dog. Puppy. Hen. </i>Bitch.<i> And then you hated her and, and just when you thought you'd got her, it, out of your whatever, then she opened her great booming rotten heart to you, caught you off bal, bal, bal, thing. </i>Ance.<i> Yeah. Thassit. Never knew where where you stood. Lay. Only one thing you were sure of, you couldn't let her go. Because, because she was yours, all you had, even in her gutters..."</i><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380334544i/693824.jpg" width="350" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> </blockquote><br>Reduced to almost-nothingness by the corruption in the city, spending his time in the gutter (literally), laughed at by everyone except his two colleagues - the thick but amiable Fred Colon and a disgrace to human race a.k.a. Nobby Nobbs - <b>Vimes gets a bit of a wake-up call when the Watch gets its newest recruit: Lance Constable Carrot Ironfoundersson,</b> a not-too-bright but earnest 6 foot 6 inches dwarf¹ <i>(he's adopted, okay?)</i> armed with a Book of Laws and Ordinances of Ankh-Morpork and a decidedly non-magical sword.<blockquote>¹<i>People who are rather more than six feet tall and nearly as broad across the shoulders often have uneventful journeys. People jump out at them from behind rocks then say things like, "Oh. Sorry. I thought you were someone else."</i>
</blockquote>And when the city is facing danger, Vimes bit by bit begins to think about it as HIS city. After all, <b>
  <i>'Things like that shouldn't be allowed to happen. Not in *my* city.'</i>
</b> <br><br>And once Vimes sets his single-minded nature to accomplish something <i>(occasionally aided by the Librarian¹, who is a peanut-loving orangutan, and the imposing figure of Lady Sybil Ramkin, who breeds swamp dragons in her spare time)</i> there is little that can stop him.<br><blockquote>¹ <i>"The three rules of the Librarians of Time and Space are: 1) Silence; 2) Books must be returned no later than the date last shown; and 3) Do not interfere with the nature of causality."</i>
</blockquote>The threat to Ankh-Morpork is real and quite substantial (<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  <i>"It's a metaphor of human bloody existence, a dragon. And if that wasn't bad enough, it's also a bloody great hot flying thing."</i>
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>), but ultimately it's brought along and fueled by the simple human greed, shallow-mindedness and jealousy, little pathetic mundanity of human existence that Pratchett so nonchalantly and non-preachily lets showcase itself. <b>He, it seems, has an excellent ability of seeing the less-than-pleasant things that make people tick - and still manages to make these sad realizations both side-splittingly and laugh-through-tears funny.</b> And I adore that.<blockquote>
<i>"Down there - he said - are people who will follow any dragon, worship any god, ignore any inequity. All out of a kind of humdrum, everyday badness. Not the really high, creative loathsomeness of the great sinners, but a sort of mass-produced darkness of the soul. Sin, you might say, without a trace of originality. They accept evil not because they say yes, but because they don't say no."</i><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380334544i/693825.jpg" width="300" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> </blockquote><br>Another prominent figure in this book, in addition to Vimes and Carrot, is, of course, <b>Lord Havelock Vetinari, the enigmatic Patrician, the tyrant who is not quite, well, tyrannical;</b> the man with his finger on the pulse of the city, the man who would not build a dungeon out of which he could not escape; <b>the man resembling a predatory flamingo, who makes Ankh Morpork work and in cynicism can greatly rival Vimes himself.</b> This is the beginning of the fascinating and not always so voluntary cooperation between Vimes and the one in power, and it's lovely to read about.<blockquote>
  <i>“You had to hand it to the Patrician, he admitted grudgingly. If you didn't, he sent men to come and take it away.”</i>
</blockquote>And, of course, some place of prominence is given to the <b>Librarian</b> <i>(the aforementioned orangutan)</i> who (no pun intended) will go bananas if you dare to call him the m-word (<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Monkey! <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>). The Librarian who knows how to navigate <b>the L-space</b>, that parallel dimension that exists between every library in the world created because of terrifying power of books.<blockquote>
  <i>"Books bend space and time. One reason the owners of those aforesaid little rambling, poky secondhand bookshops always seem slightly unearthly is that many of them really are, having strayed into this world after taking a wrong turning in their own bookshops in worlds where it is considered commendable business practice to wear carpet slippers all the time and open your shop only when you feel like it."<br><br>"There are many horrible sights in the multiverse. Somehow, though, to a soul attuned to the subtle rhythms of a library, there are few worse sights than a hole where a book ought to be."</i>
</blockquote>-----------------<br><b>I highly recommend this book to anyone as a perfect starting point on their Discworld journey.</b> It is a perfect introduction to the world of intelligent humor peppered with allusions to almost everything you can think of and smart conclusions that make you think and even ask the uncomfortable questions of yourself and the society. It is a lovely way to spend several hours curled up in a chair on a rainy day, mesmerized by Pratchett's wit and wisdom. The audiobook version of it is wonderful, immersing you in the atmosphere of the story incredibly well. For all of it, I give it <b>5 stars</b> - and, without much further ado embark on the rest on my Ankh Morpork City Watch reread.<br><blockquote> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380334544i/693826._SY540_.jpg" width="350" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><i>"They may be called the Palace Guard, the City Guard, or the Patrol. Whatever the name, their purpose in any work of heroic fantasy is identical: it is, round about Chapter Three (or ten minutes into the film) to rush into the room, attack the hero one at a time, and be slaughtered. No one ever asks them if they want to.<br><br>This book is dedicated to those fine men."</i>
</blockquote><br>—————<br><u>2020 reread (audio)</u>:<br><br>Still wonderful, and gets even better with every revisit. The audio is rather good, although I do prefer Stephen Briggs as a narrator. I don’t think I’ll ever get tired of Pratchett’s storytelling and sense of humor. He understood people so well that it’s almost unsettling. Rest In Peace, Sir Terry. I miss you.<br><br>————<br>My ever-expanding collection of Pratchett’s Discworld reviews:<br>- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/101060202" rel="nofollow noopener">Guards! Guards!</a> <br>- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/101060297" rel="nofollow noopener">Men at Arms</a><br>- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/101060304" rel="nofollow noopener">Thud!</a><br>- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/157609988" rel="nofollow noopener">Lords and Ladies</a><br>- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/160703976" rel="nofollow noopener">The Wee Free Men</a><br>- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/101060281" rel="nofollow noopener">Hogfather</a><br>- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/312984829" rel="nofollow noopener">Monstrous Regiment</a></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>

<div id="review_2320522864" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2320522864" />
    <a title="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/78485297-mario-the-lone-bookwolf-semi-reviewing-hiatus"><img alt="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588320656p2/78485297.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2320522864?book_show_action=true">Mar 07, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " href="/user/show/78485297-mario-the-lone-bookwolf-semi-reviewing-hiatus">Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/78485297-mario-the-lone-bookwolf-semi-reviewing-hiatus?shelf=pratchett-terry">pratchett-terry</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2320522864" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1623128223181651748">„Hey officer, may you please not help me! Stay away, don´t investigate, please, we´ll do traditional, good old fashioned lynch justice with far fewer victims and collateral damages. Not necessary to call reinforcement. No, stop, my insurance doesn´t cover police incompetence reinduced destruction.“<br/><br/>There are certain stereotypes surrounding police, military, and manly militia, all to take with a grain of rice, but in many aspects true, especially if a lack of government funding, missing selection </span>
  <span id="freeText1623128223181651748" style="display:none">„Hey officer, may you please not help me! Stay away, don´t investigate, please, we´ll do traditional, good old fashioned lynch justice with far fewer victims and collateral damages. Not necessary to call reinforcement. No, stop, my insurance doesn´t cover police incompetence reinduced destruction.“<br /><br />There are certain stereotypes surrounding police, military, and manly militia, all to take with a grain of rice, but in many aspects true, especially if a lack of government funding, missing selection procedures, and escalating crime lead to a mixture or cynic, weird, up to just evil racist law enforcement agencies. <br /><br />Here comes the City Watch series, circling around Captain Vimes, a patchwork rug of crime, thriller, and hard boiled detective protagonist characteristics, who goes through one of the longest character transformations, similar to Tiffany Aching. I am, at the moment, not really a friend of thrillers and the crime genre, but readers strongly invested in such literature might find much more innuendos and connotations. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Carrot Ironfoundersson brings fresh wind and blows away the nihilistic pessimism with his enthusiastic love for justice. How he reanimates the bumming, dysfunctional, grey area guards and combines his carrot and stick policy with wisdom, gives Ank Morpork hope for the dawn of a new age.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Lord Vetinari is such an eclectic, badass, clinical antihero protagonist that it´s difficult to point the finger on if he´s evil, good, or just so extremely opportunistic and finetuning his plans to perfection that he can go with the citizens mental flow and preventively manipulate and indoctrinate. He is using his fine senses, and perfect surveillance state methods, to see trends and uses whatever good or evil methods to stay in power, a truly great Machiavellian sociopath leader.<br /><br />What can be a hotter hobby than dragon breeding and thereby showing the crudeness and indirect woof woof elitism and pure breed racism that once was decent enough for humans too. Like so many harmless hobbies, the most flow intensive ones can escalate quickly and lead to absorbing most of free social and private life and time. Just take me, I´m doing nothing than reading and going out in nature in my free time for over 1 and a half decades now and it´s perfectly fine and totally normal, hehe. <br /><br />But back to the dragons, these creatures have very different characteristics, depending on use in YA, dark, classic, comedy, setting and are hardly something to make fun of or with. But, what else, Pratchett surrounds them with eccentric characters and implements them as a long time running gag. And I want to have one too.<br /><br />To reintegrate monarchy in a more or less functional system, some conservatives have the ridiculous plan <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[to use some magic, they think they have in control, which escalates to epic failing and becoming victims instead.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> Pratchett uses the scenery to show the ridiculousness of despotic government systems, especially the not only representative and powerless marionette kings, queens, and fairies, but the brutal enforcement behind them through the shadow government and hierarchies, making many monarchies the worst places to live. <br /><br />Completely not understandable and facepalmy, people have the tribal roots that make them susceptible for admiration of any kind of stupid representative, be it a democratic voted, a self declared, inherited, or couped one. This having something to see and listen to is so much easier than thinking for themselves, that they prefer it and ignore or don´t even know about all the consequences of what is really going on while a smiling or shouting or hate trolling president, chancellor, or God emperor tells them what to do. The metaphor Pratchett uses to show what can happen in such cases should be internalized.<br /><br />Tropes show how literature is conceptualized and created and which mixture of elements makes works and genres unique: <br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://tvtropes.org/pmwiki/pmwiki.php/Literature/GuardsGuards">https://tvtropes.org/pmwiki/pmwiki.ph...</a> <br /><br />This one is added to all Pratchettian reviews:<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scheibenwelt-Romane#Liste_der_Romane">https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scheibe...</a> <br />The idea of the dissected motifs rocks, highlighting the main real world inspirational elements of fiction and satire is something usually done with so called higher literature, but a much more interesting field in readable literature, as it offers the joy of reading, subtle criticism, and feeling smart all together.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1623128223181651748" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <a title="Lyn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn"><img alt="Lyn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495541962p2/5253785.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1894853309?book_show_action=true">Feb 04, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lyn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lyn" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn">Lyn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/5280986-guards-guards">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1894853309" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15824414943791486240">This is my favorite Discworld book.<br/><br/>I submit that the best way to rate a Discworld book is with a smile meter. As you read, imagine that there is a meter whose arrow jumps when you smile, moves further right when you chuckle and spikes into the red eleven when you laugh out loud.<br/><br/>Pratchett’s 1989 Discworld novel (his 8th in the series and 1st of the City Watch sub-series) made me smile all the way through the book, chuckle frequently and laugh out loud two or three times.<br/><br/>Set in Ankh-Morpork (inspi</span>
  <span id="freeText15824414943791486240" style="display:none">This is my favorite Discworld book.<br><br>I submit that the best way to rate a Discworld book is with a smile meter. As you read, imagine that there is a meter whose arrow jumps when you smile, moves further right when you chuckle and spikes into the red eleven when you laugh out loud.<br><br>Pratchett’s 1989 Discworld novel (his 8th in the series and 1st of the City Watch sub-series) made me smile all the way through the book, chuckle frequently and laugh out loud two or three times.<br><br>Set in Ankh-Morpork (inspired by Fritz Leiber’s Lankhmar?) the City Watch is composed of those night watchmen who call out at midnight that “All’s well” and who would not want to catch anyone or run after someone too fast to avoid ruining the evening with an arrest. That is until six foot six dwarf foundling Carrot joins the force as a lance constable.<br><br>This novel also features dragons, described as only Sir Terry could. Long thought extinct, the draco nobilis was just somewhere else until a group of hooded and cowled miscreants steal a book from the Library (and YES! the left turn Clyde Orangutan Librarian is featured – perhaps my second favorite Discworld character) and summon one back to Ankh-Morpork. Things do not go as planned and the rest is Pratchett magic.<br><br>As my esteemed reviewer colleague Nataliya noted in her <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/101060202?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">excellent review</a>, the best part of Pratchett’s writing is that he depicts realistic human failings in a comic setting that softens the blow of the all too real criticism in a playful way. The Discworld is a lot of fun but it is also a comedic examination of humanity and civilization and it takes a master of Pratchett’s genius talent to pull it off successfully.<br><br>A MUST read for Discword fans and would actually be a great introduction to the series for any reader.<br><br>** 2019 - I've read them all and this still my favorite.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1560093343i/27642652._SY540_.jpg" width="283" height="400" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="15824414943791486240" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2837463544" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2837463544" />
    <a title="Kevin Kuhn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59568642-kevin-kuhn"><img alt="Kevin Kuhn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508679965p2/59568642.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2837463544?book_show_action=true">Jun 03, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kevin Kuhn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kevin Kuhn" href="/user/show/59568642-kevin-kuhn">Kevin Kuhn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59568642-kevin-kuhn?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2837463544" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15855432817687301888">Reading Terry Pratchett’s “Guards! Guards!” was a joy for me. I don’t know why it took me so long to read a Discworld book, I’m a bit ashamed. I am a sucker for English humor, always have been. The good news is that I now have 40 more Discworld books to read. Honest reveal - I think I resisted because people said he was better than Douglas Adams and that rubbed me the wrong way. My view after reading one book, they are clearly in the same class, and while I still like Adams better that might be </span>
  <span id="freeText15855432817687301888" style="display:none">Reading Terry Pratchett’s “Guards! Guards!” was a joy for me. I don’t know why it took me so long to read a Discworld book, I’m a bit ashamed. I am a sucker for English humor, always have been. The good news is that I now have 40 more Discworld books to read. Honest reveal - I think I resisted because people said he was better than Douglas Adams and that rubbed me the wrong way. My view after reading one book, they are clearly in the same class, and while I still like Adams better that might be because I’m a bigger Science Fiction fan than Fantasy. In any case, it doesn’t matter, both are hilarious and talented. They both have layered humor and sneak in some wonderful insights on life.<br /><br />Pratchett dedicated this book to Palace/City Guards/Patrols, noting that no one ever asks them if they want to rush into a room, one at a time to attack the hero. It’s this trope that the story is build upon. It’s the eighth book in the Discworld series and was published in 1989. Pratchett deftly takes on all sorts of humor; sarcasm, burlesque, hyperbolic, juvenile, dark, etc., etc. He pokes at the Fantasy genre, breaks writing walls, and displays crafty, crafty wit. Like Adams, it’s largely non-stop through the entire story, but somehow the story still moves along with great pace. There are quotable quotes on nearly every page. But I didn’t just enjoy the humor, I enjoyed the fun story, and I enjoyed Pratchett’s musing on evil, government, and life, in general. The only thing Pratchett keeps sacred is books and libraries, all else is fair game for biting sarcasm!<br /><br />I think you at least need to be a passing fantasy reader to appreciate his books, and you must be tolerant of some occasional silliness, but if you pass those two simple hurdles, I can’t imagine not enjoying this book. A fun and entertaining romp through a twisted fantasy world that will make you laugh, but stealthily remind you of the underdog’s plight against the ridiculous rules of the, well, ruling class. Ahem.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15855432817687301888" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1228734994" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1228734994" />
    <a title="Apatt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt"><img alt="Apatt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349239948p2/2457095.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1228734994?book_show_action=true">Mar 15, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Apatt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Apatt" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt">Apatt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1228734994" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5000195438889447511">At the time of writing, Terry Pratchett passed away about a week ago. Beside making me sad, the news also triggered a sudden urge to read a Discworld book which needed to be satisfied immediately. I haven't read any Terry Pratchett books for years. I have always liked them but there are just so many books in the world and you know how it is, one thing led to another and somehow they didn't lead back to Sir Terry. In the meantime, I have been reading many inferior “flavor of the month” books like</span>
  <span id="freeText5000195438889447511" style="display:none">At the time of writing, Terry Pratchett passed away about a week ago. Beside making me sad, the news also triggered a sudden urge to read a Discworld book which needed to be satisfied immediately. I haven't read any Terry Pratchett books for years. I have always liked them but there are just so many books in the world and you know how it is, one thing led to another and somehow they didn't lead back to Sir Terry. In the meantime, I have been reading many inferior “flavor of the month” books like <i>The Martian</i>. I have been doing myself a disservice really.<br><br><i>Guards! Guards!</i> is one of the most popular Discworld books and I have not read it, this makes it the ideal candidate for a reentry into this unique and wonderful series. Anyway it is nice to be back in Ankh-Morpork, the most chaotic city in fantasy fiction. At the most basic level <i>Guards! Guards!</i> is Pratchett’s take on the police procedural, but his imagination is too immense to constrain the book to just a single genre parody so there is much more to this book than just the City Watch, Ankh-Morpork’s feeble excuse for a police force. The Watch mean are happily doing a crappy job, which basically involve ringing bells at certain times of the night to announce that all is well in the city. It takes the arrival of an extremely honest and straight-laced human orphan raised by dwarves to remind the Watchmen what they are supposed to be; and a huge dragon summoned from another dimension burning up the citizens and half the city to spur them into action.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1497153296i/22993153._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><a href="http://sharksden.deviantart.com/art/Guards-Guards-2-0-309124015" rel="nofollow noopener">Art by SharksDen</a><br><br>One reason I only read Terry Pratchett occasionally is that I personally prefer books where humour is a minor element of the story rather than placed at the front and centre. After reading <i>Guards! Guards!</i> I have to admit this is my misconception of what Pratchett was doing with the Discworld series (after the first few straightforward fantasy parody books). The humour takes centre stage but underneath – not even <i>far</i> underneath – Pratchett was using Discworld as a mirror to explore, lampoon and critique our world with its many ills and injustice. Certainly <i>Guards! Guards!</i> explores the theme of the policemen’s duty and honor against personal safety and interest, what makes a good cop and a bad cop so to speak. He also skewers the masses’ tendency to blindly accept whoever has the biggest gun (or fire breathing talent). These and other serious issues are explored without ever missing a humorous beat. The book is a laugh fest from beginning to end, I don’t think there is a single page that did not make me at least chuckle.<br> <br>I actually laughed out loud several times, my favorite joke involves the phrase “Bjorn Stronginthearm is my uncle” which highlights the bizarreness of the British phrase “Bob’s your uncle”. Also the patrician’s questioning of dragons’ penchant for sleeping on a huge pile of gold instead of a comfy mattress is brilliant. Don’t worry I have not spoiled the book for you, I am barely scratching the surface of the many ingenious satires, jokes and witticisms to be found in this book.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1497153840i/22993185._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br>Captain Vimes (no idea who the artist is, sorry)<br><br>The characterization of the protagonists and antagonists is also very strong. Captain Vimes is silly and funny yet flawed, sympathetic, honorable and extremely likable. I am not surprised he one of Pratchett’s most beloved characters. The formidable Patrician Vetinari is another extremely vivid and lively creation, in a TV adaptation of another Discworld book he is perfectly portrayed by Charles Dance so you can imagine the dry wit and suavity of the man. His method of escaping from captivity in a dungeon is pure evil genius.<br><br><i>Guards! Guards!</i> is effortlessly a five-star book and if I may leave you with a quote from this book that works perfectly even out of context:<br><br><i>“The reason that clichés become clichés is that they are the hammers and screwdrivers in the toolbox of communication.”</i><br><br>The irony is that there is not one cliché in this book, unless you count the ones that Pratchett turned on their heads.<br><br>R.I.P. Sir Terry, you were the best.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1427097630i/14198712.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br>Credit <a href="http://celticwren.deviantart.com/art/AT-LAST-SIR-TERRY-521868324" rel="nofollow noopener">celticwren</a><br><br><b>Note:</b> <a href="http://www.tor.com/2013/04/27/terry-pratchetts-discworld-might-be-the-highest-form-of-literature-on-the-planet/" rel="nofollow noopener">Terry Pratchett’s Discworld Might Be The Highest Form of Literature on the Planet</a> - by Brandon Sanderson</span>
  <a data-text-id="5000195438889447511" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="carol. " class="user" itemprop="url" name="carol. " href="/user/show/3978225-carol">carol. </a>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3978225-carol?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3978225-carol?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1379521823" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5904510148743445584">First of all, my site has the videos that go with this: <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://clsiewert.wordpress.com/2015/09/03/guardsguards/'>https://clsiewert.wordpress.com/2015/...</a><br/><br/>But here's the lite version:<br/><br/>My Terry Pratchett Experience ™ is remarkably similar to my Month Python Experience.™ Fun and funny, but really best done in small doses. Say, 15 minute doses if I’m watching, longer if I’m reading. <br/><br/>It turns out, I haven’t read Guards!Guards! before, although I thought I had. Quite possibly, it is because I had not. Quite possibly, it’s because I had and forgot, but you’d t</span>
  <span id="freeText5904510148743445584" style="display:none">First of all, my site has the videos that go with this: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://clsiewert.wordpress.com/2015/09/03/guardsguards/">https://clsiewert.wordpress.com/2015/...</a><br><br>But here's the lite version:<br><br>My Terry Pratchett Experience ™ is remarkably similar to my Month Python Experience.™ Fun and funny, but really best done in small doses. Say, 15 minute doses if I’m watching, longer if I’m reading. <br><br>It turns out, I haven’t read Guards!Guards! before, although I thought I had. Quite possibly, it is because I had not. Quite possibly, it’s because I had and forgot, but you’d think I’d remember the dragons. For future reference, it’s the one where the adopted dwarf Carrot goes to the big city of Ankh-Morpork and joins the Night Watch in hopes of Becoming a Man. Night Watch captain Sam Vines spends most of his days and nights in an alcoholic stupor, but the antics of Carrot in Enforcing Law and Order soon force Vines into involvement. Meanwhile, a secret society (the Elucidated Bethren of the Ebon Night) has decided to summon a dragon, intending on overthrowing the Patrician Lord Vetinari and replacing him with an old-fashioned monarchy. Some of the Brothers are quite sure they are being held down and need a monarchy to make things right:<br><b><br>“I get oppressed all the time,” said Brother Doorkeeper. “Mister Critchley, where I work, he oppresses me morning, noon and night, shouting at me and everything. And the woman in the vegetable shop, she oppresses me all the time.”<br></b><br><br> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1441328962i/16094606._SX540_.jpg" width="300" height="200" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>Like Monty Python, Pratchett specializes in absurdity, in mocking our perceptions, definitions and expectation. Clever and funny; a little bit of social commentary with an edge, both are particularly skilled in word games.<br><b><br>“‘But you’re my kind,’ said Carrot desperately. ‘In a manner of speaking, yes,’ said his father. ‘In another manner of speaking, which is a rather more precise and accurate manner of speaking, no.'”<br></b><br>Which puts me in mind of another famous sketch based on, you know, meaning and such:<br><br>“I wish to complain about this parrot I purchased not half an hour ago from this very boutique."<br>“What’s wrong with it?”<br>“It’s dead.”<br>“No, no; it’s resting.”<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1441328962i/16094607._SX540_.jpg" width="300" height="200" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>It is funny, but when it’s all clever wordplay–mocking villains in their thick dark cowls, and the general populace for being sheep, and Carrot for being So Earnest, and Lady Sybil for being such a hearty, large Englishwoman, and the only one who is really clever is the Patrician–well, it’s a bit hard to empathize. And honestly, rather tiring.<br><br>Then there’s the Librarian, an orangutan who runs the greatest library in the world. He’s trying to make Constable Carrot understand a serious crime has been committed:<br><b><br>“‘Ook.“<br><br>‘A book has been taken. A book has been taken? You summoned the Watch,’ Carrot drew himself up proudly, ‘because someone’s taken a book? You think that’s worse than murder?’<br><br>The librarian gave him the kind of look other people would reserve for people who said things like, ‘What’s so bad about genocide?’”<br></b><br>Which pretty much reminds me of the Albatross sketch. Both use the device of the straight man for maximum silliness:<br><br>The Albatross at Intermission:<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1441328962i/16094608._SX540_.jpg" width="300" height="200" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>At the end of the day, certainly fun. There’s certainly messages and social commentary that elevate it above simple romps, but it tends to be applied with heavy emphasis. Like Python, best enjoyed in short sketches. <br><br>Don’t worry, I’ll show myself out.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1441328962i/16094609.jpg" width="300" height="200" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>On the way to the Ministry of Silly Walks</span>
  <a data-text-id="5904510148743445584" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_571288240" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/571288240" />
    <a title="Bradley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley"><img alt="Bradley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508811508p2/4213258.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/571288240?book_show_action=true">Mar 26, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bradley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bradley" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley">Bradley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=2018-shelf">2018-shelf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer571288240" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer864753293573580751">Dragons and kings and cross-dressing night watch. What can go wrong?<br/><br/>Meet Terry Pratchett, who can turn anything, and I mean, anything, on its head. :)<br/><br/>On this re-read, what I remember to be a less funny book than the Watch novels that came after it suddenly becomes a rich and nostalgic ride including dwarf bread, or in this case, CAKE. Decent, law-abiding folk versus the deeply corrupt populace. And don't just ask Mr. Cut Me Own Throat. He's suspicious.<br/><br/>I love all the tropes and the way Pratchett </span>
  <span id="freeText864753293573580751" style="display:none">Dragons and kings and cross-dressing night watch. What can go wrong?<br /><br />Meet Terry Pratchett, who can turn anything, and I mean, anything, on its head. :)<br /><br />On this re-read, what I remember to be a less funny book than the Watch novels that came after it suddenly becomes a rich and nostalgic ride including dwarf bread, or in this case, CAKE. Decent, law-abiding folk versus the deeply corrupt populace. And don't just ask Mr. Cut Me Own Throat. He's suspicious.<br /><br />I love all the tropes and the way Pratchett deals with them. The whole novel is tongue-in-cheek and it's a slight bit more delightful (IMHO) than all the rest of the novels that came before it. Indeed, it's this one that sets the tone for all the rest. <br /><br />And so the transformation of Ankh-Morpork commences. :)<br /><br />Upping this by a star. </span>

          </span>

<div id="review_160498810" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/160498810" />
    <a title="Jen/The Tolkien Gal/ジェニファー" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4919283-jen-the-tolkien-gal"><img alt="Jen/The Tolkien Gal/ジェニファー" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1545334105p2/4919283.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/160498810?book_show_action=true">Apr 12, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jen/The Tolkien Gal/ジェニファー" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jen/The Tolkien Gal/ジェニファー" href="/user/show/4919283-jen-the-tolkien-gal">Jen/The Tolkien Gal/ジェニファー</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4919283-jen-the-tolkien-gal?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer160498810" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer449962334075801288"><i>“A good bookshop is just a genteel Black Hole that knows how to read.”</i><br/><br/>I always enjoyed Terry Pratchett's books - I have ever since I was about 13. But when this book came up, well - I gained even more respect for Mr Pratchett, accompanied by a bellyful of giggles and such a strong love for motley crew that is <b>The Watch</b><br/><br/>Featuring <b>Sam Vimes</b>, the grumpiest man in all Discworld:<br/><br/><i>“It wasn't by eliminating the impossible that you got at the truth, however improbable; it was by the much harder process of</i></span>
  <span id="freeText449962334075801288" style="display:none"><i>“A good bookshop is just a genteel Black Hole that knows how to read.”</i><br><br>I always enjoyed Terry Pratchett's books - I have ever since I was about 13. But when this book came up, well - I gained even more respect for Mr Pratchett, accompanied by a bellyful of giggles and such a strong love for motley crew that is <b>The Watch</b><br><br>Featuring <b>Sam Vimes</b>, the grumpiest man in all Discworld:<br><br><i>“It wasn't by eliminating the impossible that you got at the truth, however improbable; it was by the much harder process of eliminating the possibilities. You worked away, patiently asking questions and looking hard at things. You walked and talked, and in your heart you just hoped like hell that some bugger's nerve'd crack and he'd give himself up.” </i><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1536500253i/26277471._SY540_.jpg" alt="Image result for samuel vimes deviantart" width="382" height="524" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>And also featuring my absolute favourite <b>Discworld romance</b> between <b>Carrot</b>, the tall, red-headed and air-headed man raised by dwarves and <b>Angua</b>, the really grumpy werewolf.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1536500253i/26277472._SY540_.jpg" alt="Image result for carrot discworld deviantart" width="240" height="353" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Other than the <b>The Witches</b> and <b>Death</b> I truly think that <b>The Watch</b> series is the best in Pratchett's lot. If you want to learn to love one of the funniest authors of our time, pick up this book. You won't regret it.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1536500253i/26277473._SX540_.jpg" alt="Image result for the watch discworld" width="628" height="353" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><i>Courtesy of Jen's mini reviews</i> <s>Since I haven't had time to read much and reviewing old books I loved.</s></span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22809267-adrian?shelf=read-again">read-again</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22809267-adrian?shelf=humorous">humorous</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2468671829" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11241382272244071283">5 outrageously funny stars ⭐️ and serious contender for my book of the month 🙄<br/><br/>Review to follow on the morrow !<br/><br/>So in my (re)view this is what you want in a Sir Terry novel, pathos, love, heroics, honesty, modesty, drunkenness, dragons (big and small), Ankh Morpork, wizards, The Librarian, CMOT Dibbler, The Shades, The Mended Drum and above all humour, lots of humour.<br/><br/>So this is the first book that deals with the City Watch. A (very) small bunch of misfits (initially 3), led by a man that's given u</span>
  <span id="freeText11241382272244071283" style="display:none">5 outrageously funny stars ⭐️ and serious contender for my book of the month 🙄<br /><br />Review to follow on the morrow !<br /><br />So in my (re)view this is what you want in a Sir Terry novel, pathos, love, heroics, honesty, modesty, drunkenness, dragons (big and small), Ankh Morpork, wizards, The Librarian, CMOT Dibbler, The Shades, The Mended Drum and above all humour, lots of humour.<br /><br />So this is the first book that deals with the City Watch. A (very) small bunch of misfits (initially 3), led by a man that's given up on life and then joined by a (wonderfully) literal 2 metre tall dwarf (who kept bumping his head in the dwarf mines).<br />When someone tries to oust the Patrician (Lord Vetinari) from his post as leader of Ankh Morpork, Vimes the leader of the Watch turns to the bottle in his top drawer for help. When this doesn't work after many years he foregoes a wee dram and actually starts investigating a case for the first time in years. With help from a bunch of swamp dragons, Lady Ramkin and the misfits in the "Watch" he tries to sort out who is responsible for the re-introduction of impossibly large dragons; who is behind the introduction of the "new King" and where has Lord Vetinari has gone.<br />The I.L. dragon has been produced by "The Elucidated Brethren of the Ebon Night" (which was very Python-esque) when the "Thuribles of Destiny" had been chastised. Needless to say there are complications ha ha.<br /><br />I could go on and on, but it is outrageously funny, fantastically written and just a brilliant novel, so read it yourself and I hope you enjoy as much as I did.</span>

<div id="review_1693741218" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1693741218" />
    <a title="Jokoloyo" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1902630-jokoloyo"><img alt="Jokoloyo" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1423473158p2/1902630.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1693741218?book_show_action=true">Dec 03, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jokoloyo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jokoloyo" href="/user/show/1902630-jokoloyo">Jokoloyo</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780552134620" href="/book/show/233679.Guards_Guards_">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1693741218" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1735409971990072897">Before <i>Guards! Guards!</i>, Discworld novels are pretty good. This book is the FIRST GREAT Discworld novel in the series.<br/><br/>The foundations of successful formula of Watch sub-series are already on this novel<a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[: <br/>1. Hilarious comic characters in Colon and Nobby (IIRC, Colon had appeared in earlier novel as a very minor character, I forget which one). I loved the dialogues between them, especially when arguing who should rescue Carrot from drowning.<br/>2. Carrot, even as a new recruit, is already</span></span>
  <span id="freeText1735409971990072897" style="display:none">Before <i>Guards! Guards!</i>, Discworld novels are pretty good. This book is the FIRST GREAT Discworld novel in the series.<br /><br />The foundations of successful formula of Watch sub-series are already on this novel<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[: <br />1. Hilarious comic characters in Colon and Nobby (IIRC, Colon had appeared in earlier novel as a very minor character, I forget which one). I loved the dialogues between them, especially when arguing who should rescue Carrot from drowning.<br />2. Carrot, even as a new recruit, is already interesting with his idealistic-conquering-realistic theme jokes.<br />3. politics, especially seeking rightful (puppet) king to replace the manipulative Patrician.<br />4. the relationships between Vimes and Sybil <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. But the most important winning recipe is the Ankh-Morpork city itself.<br /><br />Ankh-Morpork changes from just a generic city in a medieval fantasy story on <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/601238.The_Colour_of_Magic__Discworld___1_" title="The Colour of Magic (Discworld, #1) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">The Colour of Magic</a> into a much much more living city on this novel. There are <i>ordinary</i> people in Ankh-Morpork now: street urchins, bartenders, low-life criminals, common palace guards, traders, workers, to name a few. These people are not just mentioned, but they have dialogues and readers could glimpse at their desires/needs.<br /><br />I think the living city of Ankh-Morpork is the main attractions of Discworld. The city is not only used in Watch sub-series, but also in other Discworld stories too. And this novel is the first appearance of a living Ankh-Morpork.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1735409971990072897" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2268203541" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2268203541" />
    <a title="Trish" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3461034-trish"><img alt="Trish" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588663568p2/3461034.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2268203541?book_show_action=true">Jan 24, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trish" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trish" href="/user/show/3461034-trish">Trish</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9780753106976" href="/book/show/1141278.Guards_Guards_">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2268203541" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10296027745291550900">This eighth <i>Discworld</i> novel was a delight!<br/><br/>I hadn't known the Night Watch until now and couldn't imagine loving any character as much as Granny Weatherwax but there we are. Sam Vimes as well as his ragtag band of misfits (Sgt. Colon, Nobby Nobbs, & Carrot Ironfounderson) are hilarious when stumbling through Ankh-Morpork that is currently beset by a gigantic fire-spitting dragon.<br/><br/>Dragons, even on the Disc, are supposed to be extinct. Well, the reader is informed that they simply went to a different</span>
  <span id="freeText10296027745291550900" style="display:none">This eighth <i>Discworld</i> novel was a delight!<br><br>I hadn't known the Night Watch until now and couldn't imagine loving any character as much as Granny Weatherwax but there we are. Sam Vimes as well as his ragtag band of misfits (Sgt. Colon, Nobby Nobbs, &amp; Carrot Ironfounderson) are hilarious when stumbling through Ankh-Morpork that is currently beset by a gigantic fire-spitting dragon.<br><br>Dragons, even on the Disc, are supposed to be extinct. Well, the reader is informed that they simply went to a different place. However, some magic from a very weird secret society (weirder than usual) brought one back onto the Disc in order for it to carry out a stereotypical true-heir-arrives-just-in-the-knick-of-time con - which, of course, goes all wrong ending with <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[the dragon becoming the King eventually <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. *lol*<br>The Night Watch once was the pride of the city - with "was" being the important bit in that sentence. So when Carrot, who was raised by dwarves and actually considers himself to be one, volunteers for duty, everyone is confused. Especially since he is a good fighter, honourable and knows all the (forgotten) rules and laws - and will enforce them. Totally unheard of in hundreds of years and doesn't go well with the different guilds in the city either. It doesn't help that dwarves (and therefore Carrot) are very literal creatures that don't know sarcasm or puns or figures of speech. *lol*<br><br>I LOVED all the twists on stories about the-one-true-King, the comments about libraries and librarians as well as Pratchett's depiction of dragons. I mean, look at this adorable "runt of the litter":<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1516829702i/24904317._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>There's a lot of action after the secret society idiots finally lose control over their "weapon", some cute entanglements with Lady Ramkin, heroism (Discworld-style), warped logic, L-space, people accepting tyranny without a fight (in fact, they are embracing it), and a lot of burst-out-loud moments (like when Carrot tries to guess what the Librarian is saying)!<br><br>Once again, there were numerous quotes I had to highlight in this book and as soon as I have the paperback (I read the audio version), I shall add even more.<br><br>Sharp societal observations, dark humour, adorable creatures, ... just bloody brilliant!</["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="10296027745291550900" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1092790597" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1092790597" />
    <a title="Melindam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36434323-melindam"><img alt="Melindam" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1484595601p2/36434323.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
